<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prosody">Prosody</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Spanish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Prosody">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Spanish is classified by its <a href="/wiki/Isochrony" title="Isochrony">rhythm</a> as a <a href="/wiki/Isochrony#syllable_timing" title="Isochrony">syllable-timed language</a>: each syllable has approximately the same duration regardless of stress.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[204]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[205]</a></sup>
</p><p>Spanish <a href="/wiki/Intonation_(linguistics)" title="Intonation (linguistics)">intonation</a> varies significantly according to dialect but generally conforms to a pattern of falling tone for declarative sentences and wh-questions (who, what, why, etc.) and rising tone for <a href="/wiki/Yes%E2%80%93no_question" title="Yes–no question">yes/no questions</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[206]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[207]</a></sup> There are no syntactic markers to distinguish between questions and statements and thus, the recognition of declarative or interrogative depends entirely on intonation.
</p><p>Stress most often occurs on any of the last three syllables of a word, with some rare exceptions at the fourth-to-last or earlier syllables. Stress tends to occur as follows:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[208]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (January 2021)">better source needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<ul><li>in words that end with a <a href="/wiki/Monophthong" title="Monophthong">monophthong</a>, on the penultimate syllable</li>
<li>when the word ends in a <a href="/wiki/Diphthong" title="Diphthong">diphthong</a>, on the final syllable.</li>
<li>in words that end with a consonant, on the last syllable, with the exception of two grammatical endings: <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">-n</i></span>, for third-person-plural of verbs, and <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">-s</i></span>, for plural of nouns and adjectives or for second-person-singular of verbs. However, even though a significant number of nouns and adjectives ending with <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">-n</i></span> are also stressed on the penult (<span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">joven</i></span>, <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">virgen</i></span>, <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">mitin</i></span>), the great majority of nouns and adjectives ending with <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">-n</i></span> are stressed on their last syllable (<span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">capitán</i></span>, <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">almacén</i></span>, <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">jardín</i></span>, <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">corazón</i></span>).</li>
<li>Preantepenultimate stress (stress on the fourth-to-last syllable) occurs rarely, only on verbs with <a href="/wiki/Clitic" title="Clitic">clitic</a> pronouns attached (e.g. <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">guardándoselos</i></span> 'saving them for him/her/them/you').</li></ul>
<p>In addition to the many exceptions to these tendencies, there are numerous <a href="/wiki/Minimal_pair" title="Minimal pair">minimal pairs</a> that contrast solely on stress such as <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">sábana</i></span> ('sheet') and <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">sabana</i></span> ('savannah'); <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">límite</i></span> ('boundary'), <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">limite</i></span> ('he/she limits') and <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">limité</i></span> ('I limited'); <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">líquido</i></span> ('liquid'), <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">liquido</i></span> ('I sell off') and <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">liquidó</i></span> ('he/she sold off').
</p><p>The orthographic system unambiguously reflects where the stress occurs: in the absence of an accent mark, the stress falls on the last syllable unless the last letter is ⟨n⟩, ⟨s⟩, or a vowel, in which cases the stress falls on the next-to-last (penultimate) syllable. Exceptions to those rules are indicated by an acute accent mark over the vowel of the stressed syllable. (See <a href="/wiki/Spanish_orthography" title="Spanish orthography">Spanish orthography</a>.)
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Speaker_population">Speaker population</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Spanish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Speaker population">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Spanish is the official, or national language in <a href="/wiki/Hispanic_America" title="Hispanic America">18 countries and one territory in the Americas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Equatorial_Guinea" title="Equatorial Guinea">Equatorial Guinea</a>. With a population of over 410 million, <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language_in_the_Americas" title="Spanish language in the Americas">Hispanophone America</a> accounts for the vast majority of Spanish speakers, of which <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a> is the most populous Spanish-speaking country. In the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>, Spanish is the <a href="/wiki/First_language" title="First language">mother tongue</a> of 8% of the population, with an additional 7% speaking it as a second language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[209]</a></sup> Additionally, Spanish is the second most spoken language in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> and is by far the most popular foreign language among students.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[210]</a></sup> In 2015, it was estimated that over 50 million Americans spoke Spanish, about 41 million of whom were native speakers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[211]</a></sup> With continued immigration and increased use of the language domestically in public spheres and media, the number of Spanish speakers in the United States is expected to continue growing over the forthcoming decades.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[212]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Dialectal_variation">Dialectal variation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Spanish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Dialectal variation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:452px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Variedades_principales_del_espa%C3%B1ol.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1400" data-file-width="2753" decoding="async" height="229" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Variedades_principales_del_espa%C3%B1ol.png/450px-Variedades_principales_del_espa%C3%B1ol.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Variedades_principales_del_espa%C3%B1ol.png/675px-Variedades_principales_del_espa%C3%B1ol.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Variedades_principales_del_espa%C3%B1ol.png/900px-Variedades_principales_del_espa%C3%B1ol.png 2x" width="450"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Variedades_principales_del_espa%C3%B1ol.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A world map attempting to identify the main dialects of Spanish.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Spanish_dialects_and_varieties" title="Spanish dialects and varieties">Spanish dialects and varieties</a></div>
<p>While being mutually intelligible, there are important variations (<a href="/wiki/Phonology" title="Phonology">phonological</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grammar" title="Grammar">grammatical</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lexicon" title="Lexicon">lexical</a>) in the spoken Spanish of the various regions of Spain and throughout the Spanish-speaking areas of the Americas.
</p><p>The variety with the most speakers is <a href="/wiki/Mexican_Spanish" title="Mexican Spanish">Mexican Spanish</a>. It is spoken by more than twenty percent of the world's Spanish speakers (more than 112 million of the total of more than 500 million, according to the table above). One of its main features is the <a href="/wiki/Vowel_reduction" title="Vowel reduction">reduction</a> or loss of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unstressed_vowel" title="Unstressed vowel">unstressed vowels</a>, mainly when they are in contact with the sound /s/.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[213]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[214]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Spain, northern dialects are popularly thought of as closer to the standard, although positive attitudes toward southern dialects have increased significantly in the last 50 years. The speech from the educated classes of Madrid is the standard variety for use on radio and television in Spain and it is indicated by many as the one that has most influenced the written standard for Spanish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215"><a href="#cite_note-215">[215]</a></sup> Central (European) Spanish speech patterns have been noted to be in the process of merging with more innovative southern varieties (including Eastern Andalusian and Murcian), as an emerging interdialectal levelled <i>koine</i> buffered between the Madrid's traditional national standard and the Seville speech trends.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[216]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Phonology_2">Phonology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Spanish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Phonology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Phonetic_change_%22f_%E2%86%92_h%22_in_Spanish" title='Phonetic change "f → h" in Spanish'>Phonetic change "f → h" in Spanish</a></div>
<p>The four main phonological divisions are based respectively on (1) the phoneme <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_dental_fricative" title="Voiceless dental fricative"><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/θ/</span></a> ("theta"), (2) the <a href="/wiki/Debuccalization" title="Debuccalization">debuccalization</a> of syllable-final <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/s/</span>, (3) the sound of the spelled ⟨s⟩, (4) and the phoneme <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Palatal_lateral_approximant" title="Palatal lateral approximant"><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ʎ/</span></a> ("turned <i>y</i>"),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[217]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>The phoneme <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/θ/</span> (spelled <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">c</i></span> before <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">e</i></span> or <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">i</i></span> and spelled ⟨z⟩ elsewhere), a <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_dental_fricative" title="Voiceless dental fricative">voiceless dental fricative</a> as in English <i><b>th</b>ing</i>, is maintained by a majority of Spain's population, especially in the northern and central parts of the country. In other areas (some parts of southern Spain, the <a href="/wiki/Canary_Islands" title="Canary Islands">Canary Islands</a>, and the Americas), <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/θ/</span> does not exist and <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/s/</span> occurs instead. The maintenance of phonemic contrast is called <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Distinci%C3%B3n" title="Distinción">distinción</a></i></span> in Spanish, while the merger is generally called <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seseo" title="Seseo">seseo</a></i></span> (in reference to the usual realization of the merged phoneme as <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[s]</span>) or, occasionally, <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ceceo" title="Ceceo">ceceo</a></i></span> (referring to its interdental realization, <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[θ]</span>, in some parts of southern Spain). In most of Hispanic America, the spelled ⟨c⟩ before ⟨e⟩ or ⟨i⟩, and spelled ⟨z⟩ is always pronounced as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Voiceless_alveolar_sibilant#Dentalized_laminal_alveolar" title="Voiceless alveolar sibilant">voiceless dental sibilant</a>.</li>
<li>The debuccalization (pronunciation as <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[h]</span>, or loss) of syllable-final <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/s/</span> is associated with the southern half of Spain and lowland Americas: Central America (except central Costa Rica and Guatemala), the Caribbean, coastal areas of southern Mexico, and South America except Andean highlands. Debuccalization is frequently called "aspiration" in English, and <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">aspiración</i></span> in Spanish. When there is no debuccalization, the syllable-final <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/s/</span> is pronounced as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Voiceless_alveolar_sibilant#Retracted_alveolar" title="Voiceless alveolar sibilant">voiceless "apico-alveolar" sibilant</a> or as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Voiceless_alveolar_sibilant#Dentalized_laminal_alveolar" title="Voiceless alveolar sibilant">voiceless dental sibilant</a> in the same fashion as in the next paragraph.</li>
<li>The sound that corresponds to the letter ⟨s⟩ is pronounced in northern and central Spain as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Voiceless_alveolar_sibilant#Retracted_alveolar" title="Voiceless alveolar sibilant">voiceless "apico-alveolar" sibilant</a> <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[s̺]</span> (also described acoustically as "<a href="/wiki/Grave_and_acute" title="Grave and acute">grave</a>" and articulatorily as "retracted"), with a weak "hushing" sound reminiscent of <a href="/wiki/Retroflex_consonant" title="Retroflex consonant">retroflex</a> fricatives. In <a href="/wiki/Andalusia" title="Andalusia">Andalusia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canary_Islands" title="Canary Islands">Canary Islands</a> and most of Hispanic America (except in the <a href="/wiki/Colombian_Spanish#Paisa_dialect" title="Colombian Spanish">Paisa region</a> of Colombia) it is pronounced as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Voiceless_alveolar_sibilant#Dentalized_laminal_alveolar" title="Voiceless alveolar sibilant">voiceless dental sibilant</a> <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[s]</span>, much like the most frequent pronunciation of the /s/ of English. Because /s/ is one of the most frequent phonemes in Spanish, the difference of pronunciation is one of the first to be noted by a Spanish-speaking person to differentiate Spaniards from Spanish-speakers of the Americas.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></li>
<li>The phoneme <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ʎ/</span>, spelled ⟨ll⟩, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Palatal_lateral_approximant" title="Palatal lateral approximant">palatal lateral</a> consonant that can be approximated by the sound of the ⟨lli⟩ of English <i>million</i>, tends to be maintained in less-urbanized areas of northern Spain and in <a href="/wiki/Andean_Spanish" title="Andean Spanish">highland areas of South America</a>. Meanwhile, in the speech of most other Spanish-speakers, it is merged with <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ʝ/</span> ("curly-tail <i>j</i>"), a non-lateral, usually voiced, usually fricative, palatal consonant, sometimes compared to English <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/j/</span> (<i>yod</i>) as in <i><b>y</b>acht</i> and spelled ⟨y⟩ in Spanish. As with other forms of allophony across world languages, the small difference of the spelled ⟨ll⟩ and the spelled ⟨y⟩ is usually not perceived (the difference is not heard) by people who do not produce them as different phonemes. Such a phonemic merger is called <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es"><a href="/wiki/Ye%C3%ADsmo" title="Yeísmo">yeísmo</a></i></span> in Spanish. In <a href="/wiki/Rioplatense_Spanish" title="Rioplatense Spanish">Rioplatense Spanish</a>, the merged phoneme is generally pronounced as a postalveolar fricative, either voiced <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ʒ]</span> (as in English <i>measure</i> or the French ⟨j⟩) in the central and western parts of the dialectal region (<span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">zheísmo</i></span>), or voiceless <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ʃ]</span> (as in the French ⟨ch⟩ or Portuguese ⟨x⟩) in and around Buenos Aires and Montevideo (<span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">sheísmo</i></span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218"><a href="#cite_note-218">[218]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Morphology">Morphology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Spanish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Morphology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The main <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(linguistics)" title="Morphology (linguistics)">morphological</a> variations between dialects of Spanish involve differing uses of pronouns, especially those of the second <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_person" title="Grammatical person">person</a> and, to a lesser extent, the <a href="/wiki/Object_pronoun" title="Object pronoun">object pronouns</a> of the third <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_person" title="Grammatical person">person</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Voseo">Voseo</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Spanish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Voseo">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>

<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Voseo-extension-real.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="922" data-file-width="694" decoding="async" height="332" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Voseo-extension-real.PNG/250px-Voseo-extension-real.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Voseo-extension-real.PNG/375px-Voseo-extension-real.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Voseo-extension-real.PNG/500px-Voseo-extension-real.PNG 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Voseo-extension-real.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An examination of the dominance and stress of the voseo feature in Hispanic America. Data generated as illustrated by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Association_of_Spanish_Language_Academies" title="Association of Spanish Language Academies">Association of Spanish Language Academies</a>. The darker the area, the stronger its dominance.</div></div></div>
<p>Virtually all dialects of Spanish make the <a href="/wiki/T%E2%80%93V_distinction" title="T–V distinction">distinction</a> between a formal and a familiar <a href="/wiki/Register_(sociolinguistics)" title="Register (sociolinguistics)">register</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_person" title="Grammatical person">second-person</a> <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_number" title="Grammatical number">singular</a> and thus have two different <a href="/wiki/Pronoun" title="Pronoun">pronouns</a> meaning "you": <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">usted</i></span> in the formal and either <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">tú</i></span> or <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">vos</i></span> in the familiar (and each of these three pronouns has its associated verb forms), with the choice of <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">tú</i></span> or <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">vos</i></span> varying from one dialect to another. The use of <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">vos</i></span> (and/or its verb forms) is called <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es"><a href="/wiki/Voseo" title="Voseo">voseo</a></i></span>. In a few dialects, all three pronouns are used, with <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">usted</i></span>, <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">tú</i></span>, and <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">vos</i></span> denoting respectively formality, familiarity, and intimacy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rae.es_site_219-0"><a href="#cite_note-rae.es_site-219">[219]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">voseo</i></span>, <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">vos</i></span> is the <a href="/wiki/Subject_(grammar)" title="Subject (grammar)">subject</a> form (<span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">vos decís</i></span>, "you say") and the form for the object of a <a href="/wiki/Preposition_and_postposition" title="Preposition and postposition">preposition</a> (<span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">voy con vos</i></span>, "I am going with you"), while the direct and indirect <a href="/wiki/Object_(grammar)" title="Object (grammar)">object</a> forms, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Possessive_adjective" title="Possessive adjective">possessives</a>, are the same as those associated with <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">tú</i></span>: <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">Vos sabés que tus amigos te respetan</i></span> ("You know your friends respect you").
</p><p>The verb forms of <i>general voseo</i> are the same as those used with <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">tú</i></span> except in the present <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_tense" title="Grammatical tense">tense</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indicative" title="Indicative">indicative</a> and <a href="/wiki/Imperative_mood" title="Imperative mood">imperative</a>) verbs. The forms for <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">vos</i></span> generally can be derived from those of <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">vosotros</i></span> (the traditional second-person familiar <i>plural</i>) by deleting the <a href="/wiki/Semivowel" title="Semivowel">glide</a> <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[i̯]</span>, or <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/d/</span>, where it appears in the ending: <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">vosotros pensá<b>i</b>s</i></span> &gt; <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">vos pensás</i></span>; <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">vosotros volvé<b>i</b>s</i></span> &gt; <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">vos volvés</i></span>, <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">pensa<b>d</b>!</i></span> (<span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">vosotros</i></span>) &gt; <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">pensá!</i></span> (<span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">vos</i></span>), <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">volve<b>d</b>!</i></span> (<span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">vosotros</i></span>) &gt; <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">volvé!</i></span> (<span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">vos</i></span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-voseo_DPD_220-0"><a href="#cite_note-voseo_DPD-220">[220]</a></sup>
</p>

<p>In Chilean <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">voseo</i></span> on the other hand, almost all verb forms are distinct from their standard <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">tú</i></span>-forms.
</p>

<p>The use of the pronoun <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">vos</i></span> with the verb forms of <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">tú</i></span> (<span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">vos piensas</i></span>) is called "pronominal <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">voseo</i></span>". Conversely, the use of the verb forms of <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">vos</i></span> with the pronoun <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">tú</i></span> (<span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">tú pensás</i></span> or <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">tú pensái</i></span>) is called "verbal <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">voseo</i></span>".<br/>
In Chile, for example, <i>verbal voseo</i> is much more common than the actual use of the pronoun <i>vos</i>, which is usually reserved for highly informal situations.
</p><p>And in Central American <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">voseo</i></span>, one can see even further distinction.
</p>

<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Distribution_in_Spanish-speaking_regions_of_the_Americas">Distribution in Spanish-speaking regions of the Americas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Spanish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Distribution in Spanish-speaking regions of the Americas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>Although <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">vos</i></span> is not used in Spain, it occurs in many Spanish-speaking regions of the Americas as the primary spoken form of the second-person singular familiar pronoun, with wide differences in social consideration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[222]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (November 2020)">better source needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Generally, it can be said that there are zones of exclusive use of <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">tuteo</i></span> (the use of <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">tú</i></span>) in the following areas: almost all of <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a>, the <a href="/wiki/West_Indies" title="West Indies">West Indies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Panama" title="Panama">Panama</a>, most of <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a>, <a href="/wiki/Venezuela" title="Venezuela">Venezuela</a> and coastal <a href="/wiki/Ecuador" title="Ecuador">Ecuador</a>.
</p><p><span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">Tuteo</i></span> as a cultured form alternates with <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">voseo</i></span> as a popular or rural form in <a href="/wiki/Bolivia" title="Bolivia">Bolivia</a>, in the north and south of Peru, in Andean Ecuador, in small zones of the Venezuelan Andes (and most notably in the Venezuelan state of <a href="/wiki/Zulia" title="Zulia">Zulia</a>), and in a large part of Colombia. Some researchers maintain that <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">voseo</i></span> can be heard in some parts of eastern Cuba, and others assert that it is absent from the island.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[223]</a></sup>
</p><p><span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">Tuteo</i></span> exists as the second-person usage with an intermediate degree of formality alongside the more familiar <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">voseo</i></span> in <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a>, in the Venezuelan state of <a href="/wiki/Zulia" title="Zulia">Zulia</a>, on the Caribbean coast of <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombia</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Azuero_Peninsula" title="Azuero Peninsula">Azuero Peninsula</a> in Panama, in the Mexican state of <a href="/wiki/Chiapas" title="Chiapas">Chiapas</a>, and in parts of Guatemala.
</p><p>Areas of generalized <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">voseo</i></span> include <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nicaragua" title="Nicaragua">Nicaragua</a>, eastern <a href="/wiki/Bolivia" title="Bolivia">Bolivia</a>, <a href="/wiki/El_Salvador" title="El Salvador">El Salvador</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guatemala" title="Guatemala">Guatemala</a>, <a href="/wiki/Honduras" title="Honduras">Honduras</a>, <a href="/wiki/Costa_Rica" title="Costa Rica">Costa Rica</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paraguay" title="Paraguay">Paraguay</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uruguay" title="Uruguay">Uruguay</a> and the Colombian departments of <a href="/wiki/Antioquia_Department" title="Antioquia Department">Antioquia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Caldas_Department" title="Caldas Department">Caldas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Risaralda_Department" title="Risaralda Department">Risaralda</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quindio" title="Quindio">Quindio</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Valle_del_Cauca" title="Valle del Cauca">Valle del Cauca</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rae.es_site_219-1"><a href="#cite_note-rae.es_site-219">[219]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ustedes">Ustedes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Spanish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Ustedes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">Ustedes</i></span> functions as formal and informal second-person plural in all of Hispanic America, the <a href="/wiki/Canary_Islands" title="Canary Islands">Canary Islands</a>, and parts of <a href="/wiki/Andalusia" title="Andalusia">Andalusia</a>. It agrees with verbs in the 3rd person plural. Most of Spain maintains the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/T-V_distinction" title="T-V distinction">formal/familiar distinction</a> with <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">ustedes</i></span> and <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">vosotros</i></span> respectively. The use of <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">ustedes</i></span> with the second person plural is sometimes heard in Andalusia, but it's non-standard.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Usted">Usted</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Spanish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Usted">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">Usted</i></span> is the usual second-person singular pronoun in a formal context, but it is used jointly with the third-person singular voice of the verb. It is used to convey respect toward someone who is a generation older or is of higher authority ("you, sir"/"you, ma'am"). It is also used in a <i>familiar</i> context by many speakers in Colombia and Costa Rica and in parts of Ecuador and Panama, to the exclusion of <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">tú</i></span> or <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">vos</i></span>. This usage is sometimes called <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es"><a class="extiw" href="https://es.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ustedeo" title="es:Ustedeo">ustedeo</a></i></span> in Spanish.
</p><p>In Central America, especially in Honduras, <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">usted</i></span> is often used as a formal pronoun to convey respect between the members of a romantic couple. <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">Usted</i></span> is also used that way between parents and children in the Andean regions of Ecuador, Colombia and Venezuela.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Third-person_object_pronouns">Third-person object pronouns</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Spanish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Third-person object pronouns">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Most speakers use (and the <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Real_Academia_Espa%C3%B1ola" title="Real Academia Española">Real Academia Española</a></i></span> prefers) the pronouns <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">lo</i></span> and <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">la</i></span> for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Direct_object#The_object_in_linguistics" title="Direct object"><i>direct</i> objects</a> (masculine and feminine respectively, regardless of <a href="/wiki/Animacy" title="Animacy">animacy</a>, meaning "him", "her", or "it"), and <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">le</i></span> for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Direct_object#The_object_in_linguistics" title="Direct object"><i>indirect</i> objects</a> (regardless of <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_gender" title="Grammatical gender">gender</a> or <a href="/wiki/Animacy" title="Animacy">animacy</a>, meaning "to him", "to her", or "to it"). The usage is sometimes called "etymological", as these direct and indirect object pronouns are a continuation, respectively, of the <a href="/wiki/Accusative_case" title="Accusative case">accusative</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dative_case" title="Dative case">dative</a> pronouns of Latin, the ancestor language of Spanish.
</p><p>Deviations from this norm (more common in Spain than in the Americas) are called "<span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es"><a href="/wiki/Le%C3%ADsmo" title="Leísmo">leísmo</a></i></span>", "<span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es"><a href="/wiki/Lo%C3%ADsmo" title="Loísmo">loísmo</a></i></span>", or "<span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/La%C3%ADsmo" title="Laísmo">laísmo</a></i></span>", according to which respective pronoun, <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">le</i></span>, <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">lo</i></span>, or <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">la</i></span>, has expanded beyond the etymological usage (<span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">le</i></span> as a direct object, or <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">lo</i></span> or <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">la</i></span> as an indirect object).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vocabulary">Vocabulary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Spanish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Vocabulary">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Some words can be significantly different in different Hispanophone countries. Most Spanish speakers can recognize other Spanish forms even in places where they are not commonly used, but Spaniards generally do not recognize specifically American usages. For example, Spanish <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">mantequilla</i></span>, <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">aguacate</i></span> and <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">albaricoque</i></span> (respectively, 'butter', 'avocado', 'apricot') correspond to <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">manteca</i></span> (word used for <a href="/wiki/Lard" title="Lard">lard</a> in <a href="/wiki/Peninsular_Spanish" title="Peninsular Spanish">Peninsular Spanish</a>), <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">palta</i></span>, and <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">damasco</i></span>, respectively, in Argentina, Chile (except <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">manteca</i></span>), Paraguay, Peru (except <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">manteca</i></span> and <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">damasco</i></span>), and Uruguay.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Relation_to_other_languages">Relation to other languages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Spanish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Relation to other languages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Comparison_of_Portuguese_and_Spanish" title="Comparison of Portuguese and Spanish">Comparison of Portuguese and Spanish</a></div>
<p>Spanish is closely related to the other <a href="/wiki/West_Iberian_languages" title="West Iberian languages">West Iberian Romance languages</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Asturian_language" title="Asturian language">Asturian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aragonese_language" title="Aragonese language">Aragonese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Galician_language" title="Galician language">Galician</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ladino_language" title="Ladino language">Ladino</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leonese_Language" title="Leonese Language">Leonese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mirandese_language" title="Mirandese language">Mirandese</a> and <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language">Portuguese</a>.
</p><p>It is generally acknowledged that Portuguese and Spanish speakers can communicate in written form, with varying degrees of mutual intelligibility.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224"><a href="#cite_note-224">[224]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225"><a href="#cite_note-225">[225]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[226]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[227]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mutual_intelligibility" title="Mutual intelligibility">Mutual intelligibility</a> of the <i>written</i> Spanish and Portuguese languages is remarkably high, and the difficulties of the spoken forms are based more on phonology than on grammatical and lexical dissimilarities. <i><a href="/wiki/Ethnologue" title="Ethnologue">Ethnologue</a></i> gives estimates of the <a href="/wiki/Lexical_similarity" title="Lexical similarity">lexical similarity</a> between related languages in terms of precise percentages. For Spanish and Portuguese, that figure is 89%. Italian, on the other hand is phonologically similar to Spanish, but has a lower lexical similarity of 82%. Mutual intelligibility between Spanish and <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a> or between Spanish and <a href="/wiki/Romanian_language" title="Romanian language">Romanian</a> is lower still, given lexical similarity ratings of 75% and 71% respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ethnologue_228-0"><a href="#cite_note-ethnologue-228">[228]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ezglot_229-0"><a href="#cite_note-ezglot-229">[229]</a></sup> Comprehension of Spanish by French speakers who have not studied the language is much lower, at an estimated 45%. In general, thanks to the common features of the writing systems of the Romance languages, interlingual comprehension of the written word is greater than that of oral communication.
</p><p>The Spanish vocabulary has been influenced by several languages: As in other European languages, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Classical_Greek" title="Classical Greek">Classical Greek</a> words (Hellenisms) are abundant in several fields, mainly in <a href="/wiki/Art" title="Art">Art</a>, <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">Science</a>, <a href="/wiki/Politics" title="Politics">Politics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nature" title="Nature">Nature</a>, etc.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230"><a href="#cite_note-230">[230]</a></sup> Its vocabulary has also been <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language_influence_on_the_Spanish_language" title="Arabic language influence on the Spanish language">influenced by Arabic</a>, having developed during the <a href="/wiki/Al-Andalus" title="Al-Andalus">Al-Andalus</a> era in the <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">Iberian Peninsula</a>, with around 8% of its vocabulary having <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a> lexical roots.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[231]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-232"><a href="#cite_note-232">[232]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-233"><a href="#cite_note-233">[233]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dworkin83_234-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dworkin83-234">[234]</a></sup> It has also been influenced by <a href="/wiki/Basque_language" title="Basque language">Basque</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iberian_language" title="Iberian language">Iberian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Celtiberian_language" title="Celtiberian language">Celtiberian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gothic_language" title="Gothic language">Visigothic</a>, and other neighboring Ibero-Romance languages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-235"><a href="#cite_note-235">[235]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dworkin83_234-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dworkin83-234">[234]</a></sup> Additionally, it has absorbed vocabulary from other languages, particularly other Romance languages such as <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>, <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">Italian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mozarabic_language" title="Mozarabic language">Mozarabic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language">Portuguese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Galician_language" title="Galician language">Galician</a>, <a href="/wiki/Catalan_language" title="Catalan language">Catalan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Occitan_language" title="Occitan language">Occitan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sardinian_language" title="Sardinian language">Sardinian</a>, as well as from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quechua_language" title="Quechua language">Quechua</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nahuatl_language" title="Nahuatl language">Nahuatl</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_Spanish_words_of_Indigenous_American_Indian_origin" title="List of Spanish words of Indigenous American Indian origin">other indigenous languages of the Americas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[236]</a></sup>
</p><p>The following table compares the forms of some common words in several Romance languages:
</p>

<td><span title="Asturian-language text"><i lang="ast"><b>nada</b></i></span><br/>(also <span title="Asturian-language text"><i lang="ast"><b>un res</b></i></span>)
</td>
<td><span title="Aragonese-language text"><i lang="an"><b>cosa</b></i></span>
</td>
<td><span title="Catalan-language text"><i lang="ca"><b>res</b></i></span>
</td>
<td><span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr">rien, nul</i></span>
</td>
<td><span title="Italian-language text"><i lang="it">niente, nulla</i></span><br/><span title="Italian-language text"><i lang="it">mica</i></span> (negative particle)
</td>
<td><span title="Romanian-language text"><i lang="ro">nimic, nul</i></span>
</td>
<td>'nothing'
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><span title="Latin-language text"><span lang="la" style="font-style: normal;"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r920966791" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="smallcaps">cāseu(m) fōrmāticu(m)</span></span></span><br/>"form-cheese"
</td>
<td><span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es"><b>queso</b></i></span>
</td>
<td><span title="Galician-language text"><i lang="gl"><b>queixo</b></i></span>
</td>
<td><span title="Portuguese-language text"><i lang="pt">queijo</i></span>
</td>
<td><span title="Asturian-language text"><i lang="ast"><b>quesu</b></i></span>
</td>
<td><span title="Aragonese-language text"><i lang="an"><b>queso</b></i></span>
</td>
<td><span title="Catalan-language text"><i lang="ca"><b>formatge</b></i></span>
</td>
<td><span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr">fromage</i></span>
</td>
<td><span title="Italian-language text"><i lang="it">formaggio/cacio</i></span>
</td>
<td><span title="Romanian-language text"><i lang="ro">caș</i></span><sup>10</sup>
</td>
<td>'cheese'
</td></tr></tbody></table>
</div>
<p><small>
1. In Romance etymology, Latin terms are given in the Accusative since most forms derive from this case.<br/>
2. As in "us very selves", an emphatic expression.<br/>
3. Also <span title="Portuguese-language text"><i lang="pt">nós outros</i></span> in early modern Portuguese (e.g. <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Lusiads" title="The Lusiads">The Lusiads</a></i>), and <span title="Galician-language text"><i lang="gl">nosoutros</i></span> in Galician.<br/>
4. Alternatively <span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr">nous autres</i></span> in <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>.<br/>
5. <span title="Italian-language text"><i lang="it">noialtri</i></span> in many Southern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_languages_of_Italy" title="List of languages of Italy">Italian dialects and languages</a>.<br/>
6. Medieval Catalan (e.g. <i><a href="/wiki/Llibre_dels_fets" title="Llibre dels fets">Llibre dels fets</a></i>).<br/>
7. Modified with the learned suffix <i>-ción</i>.<br/>
8. Depending on the written norm used (see <a href="/wiki/Reintegrationism" title="Reintegrationism">Reintegrationism</a>).<br/>
9. From <a href="/wiki/Basque_language" title="Basque language">Basque</a> <i>esku</i>, "hand" + <i>erdi</i>, "half, incomplete". Notice that this negative meaning also applies for Latin <i>sinistra(m)</i> ("dark, unfortunate").<br/>
10. Romanian <i>caș</i> (from Latin <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r920966791" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="smallcaps">cāsevs</span>) means a type of cheese. The universal term for cheese in Romanian is <i>brânză</i> (from unknown etymology).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-237"><a href="#cite_note-237">[237]</a></sup>
</small>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Judaeo-Spanish">Judaeo-Spanish</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Spanish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Judaeo-Spanish">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Judaeo-Spanish" title="Judaeo-Spanish">Judaeo-Spanish</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rashiscript.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="87" data-file-width="341" decoding="async" height="56" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Rashiscript.PNG/220px-Rashiscript.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Rashiscript.PNG/330px-Rashiscript.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/04/Rashiscript.PNG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rashiscript.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Rashi_script" title="Rashi script">Rashi script</a>, originally used to print Judaeo-Spanish.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:192px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Delacroix_letter.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="618" data-file-width="712" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Delacroix_letter.png/190px-Delacroix_letter.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Delacroix_letter.png/285px-Delacroix_letter.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Delacroix_letter.png/380px-Delacroix_letter.png 2x" width="190"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Delacroix_letter.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An original letter in Haketia, written in 1832.</div></div></div>
<p>Judaeo-Spanish, also known as Ladino,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ladino_238-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ladino-238">[238]</a></sup> is a variety of Spanish which preserves many features of medieval Spanish and Portuguese and is spoken by descendants of the <a href="/wiki/Sephardi_Jews" title="Sephardi Jews">Sephardi Jews</a> who were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alhambra_decree" title="Alhambra decree">expelled from Spain in the 15th century</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ladino_238-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ladino-238">[238]</a></sup> Conversely, in Portugal the vast majority of the Portuguese Jews converted and became 'New Christians'. Therefore, its relationship to Spanish is comparable with that of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yiddish_language" title="Yiddish language">Yiddish language</a> to <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>. Ladino speakers today are almost exclusively <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sephardim" title="Sephardim">Sephardi</a> Jews, with family roots in Turkey, Greece, or the Balkans, and living mostly in Israel, Turkey, and the United States, with a few communities in Hispanic America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ladino_238-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ladino-238">[238]</a></sup> Judaeo-Spanish lacks the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amerindian_languages" title="Amerindian languages">Native American vocabulary</a> which was acquired by standard Spanish during the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Empire" title="Spanish Empire">Spanish colonial period</a>, and it retains many archaic features which have since been lost in standard Spanish. It contains, however, other vocabulary which is not found in standard Spanish, including vocabulary from <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">Hebrew</a>, French, Greek and <a href="/wiki/Turkish_language" title="Turkish language">Turkish</a>, and other languages spoken where the Sephardim settled.
</p><p>Judaeo-Spanish is in serious danger of extinction because many native speakers today are elderly as well as elderly <i>olim</i> (immigrants to <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>) who have not transmitted the language to their children or grandchildren. However, it is experiencing a minor revival among Sephardi communities, especially in music. In Latin American communities, the danger of extinction is also due to assimilation by modern Spanish.
</p><p>A related dialect is <a href="/wiki/Haketia" title="Haketia">Haketia</a>, the Judaeo-Spanish of northern Morocco. This too tended to assimilate with modern Spanish, during the Spanish occupation of the region.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Writing_system">Writing system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Spanish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Writing system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
